<div id="review_432057204" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/432057204" />
    <a title="Lawyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer"><img alt="Lawyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603672493p2/3025547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/432057204?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawyer" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer">Lawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lawyer by: </span>
      October 2012 group read "On The Southern Literary Trail"
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=courage">courage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=on-the-southern-literary-trail">on-the-southern-literary-trail</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=responsibility">responsibility</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=southern-literature">southern-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=sexuality">sexuality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=southern-class-and-culture">southern-class-and-culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=contemporary-southern-literature">contemporary-southern-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=crime">crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer432057204" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3773798750184080757"><b>Winter's Bone: Daniel Woodrell's Tale of When Blood is thicker than water</b><br/><br/>When I was a boy we had no Interstates. The car was not air-conditioned. A trip from Tuscaloosa to North Alabama was a twisting, turning drive through mountains and steep valleys as you drove into the northern part of the County. We traveled early to avoid the afternoon heat. The mists rose up from the valleys making the mountains look as though they grew out of clouds. My grandfather would comment on the smell of the worki</span>
  <span id="freeText3773798750184080757" style="display:none"><b>Winter's Bone: Daniel Woodrell's Tale of When Blood is thicker than water</b><br><br>When I was a boy we had no Interstates. The car was not air-conditioned. A trip from Tuscaloosa to North Alabama was a twisting, turning drive through mountains and steep valleys as you drove into the northern part of the County. We traveled early to avoid the afternoon heat. The mists rose up from the valleys making the mountains look as though they grew out of clouds. My grandfather would comment on the smell of the working stills hidden in the country we passed through. Although prohibition had ended decades before, many counties, ruled by Bible thumping Southern Baptists and Methodists voted to keep their Counties free of liquor. That Jesus turned water into wine seemed to have little influence on them.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=WalkerCountyWoodlands.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380393986i/758592._SX540_.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>North of Tuscaloosa Co., Alabama</i><br><br>When I became a man and a career prosecutor, violation of the prohibited liquors statutes were few and far between. As time passed one drug after another became the most desired. But nothing compared to methamphetamine and it's staying power, and the growing volume of cases that crossed my desk.<br><br>I've met cookers, dealers and users. Users tell me that Meth gave them the greatest sex they ever had and they kept looking for the same big bang with each time they used.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=meth-addiction-pics.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380393986i/758593.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>I just can't get no satisfaction...Before and After on Meth</i> <br><br>That land up north of the County is still there, though I usually bypass it by taking the Interstate now. And I can't remember the last time I was in a dry County. But that country up there sounds a lot like the setting of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112525.Winter_s_Bone" title="Winter's Bone by Daniel Woodrell" rel="nofollow noopener">Winter's Bone</a>. The people up that way are a lot a like, too. They don't talk much, especially if you're the Law or you work with the Law. I worked two killings where the bodies both ended up on the Tiger Mine Strip Pit Road. It's a God forsaken place. And by the time you find a crime scene, any car involved has been stripped and burned, and the blow flies and maggots and just about anything that walks crawls or flies has turned what was a living human being into a mess of stinking goo. That trick of putting Vick's in your nose works a little bit, but the smell of death gets into your hair, mustache, and clothes.<br><br>There's always the guilty and always the innocent. It's the innocents that got left behind that always worried me the most.<br><br>In the early Meth days, cookers hadn't got their chemistry down real well. It wasn't unusual that a cooker blew himself and his lab sky high. The place stunk to high Heaven. Not even we knew how dangerous the fumes were when we went into one of the places. But in my line of work you developed a black sense of humor. Dang. Another one got it wrong. No file to open. Breaks your heart, don't it. Yep. Sure does. Reckon he's playin' his harp. Naw. He's tunin' his fork.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=meth_explosion.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380393986i/758594.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>After a meth lab explosion</i><br><br>Daniel Woodrell has written a book that I identify with on a number of levels. It's my first Woodrell. But it won't be the last. And I won't forget this book for a long, long time. Frankly, I didn't think I needed to read about a Meth cooker. However, by page four I realized Woodrell didn't care about the cooker anymore than I did. This is about the innocents that get left behind and how they must get by, <i>if</i> they manage to get by at all.<br><br>And this is when Woodrell hooked me: <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"Walnuts were still falling when Ree saw him last. Walnuts were thumping to ground in the night like stalking footsteps of some large thing that never quite came into view, and Jessup had paced on this porch in a worried slouch, dented nose snuffling, lantern jaw smoked by beard, eyes uncertain and alarmed by each walnut thump. The darkness and those thumps out in the darkness seemed to keep him jumpy. He paced until a decision popped into his head, then started down the steps, going fast into the night before his mind could change. He said, 'Start lookin' for me soon as you see my face. 'Til then, don't even wonder."</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br>In a few terse sentences, Daniel Woodrell has introduced you to Jessup Dolly. Dolly is telling his seventeen year old daughter, Ree, goodbye. Dolly is a man on the run. He's the best Meth cooker in the Missouri Ozarks. The law has caught up with him. Jessup has done one stretch in the pen. He doesn't want to do another. He's out on bond, putting up his family's home and timberland. He has a court appearance in a week. He doesn't tell his daughter they're going to lose their home.<br><br>Jessup leaves behind a wife, either insane or in an advancing stage of dementia, two boys, and his seventeen year old daughter Ree. When you're a meth cooker's daughter you grow up hard and you grow up fast. Ree left school at sixteen to care for her mother and two younger brothers, Sonny and Harold. <br><br>No gas for the chainsaw? Ree chops wood for the potbelly stove with the ease of a lumberjack. No food on the table? Ree can bark a squirrel flattened against a tree limb with a .22 bullet. She rarely misses. Ammunition costs money.<br><br>Ree's got plenty of family. Jessup's brother, Tear Drop, named for a penitentiary tattoo, the Miltons, and the Halsam's. Pretty much everybody is kin through some degree of marriage or cousins, removed by generation or not.<br><br>Ree is a woman in an adolescent's body. She has satisfied her sexual curiosity, exploring pleasure with her girl friend Gail Lockrum. She knows how to kiss, but is disappointed with her first kiss with a boy when she asks for his tongue and he responds, "Yuck." Her first experience is with a doper friend of her father, Little Arthur. He gave her mushrooms and told her it would make her sandwich taste much better. She feels all ooey gooey and wonders if she had only imagined it until she found her panties ripped. Yeah, a doper's daughter grows up hard and fast.<br><br>When the bondsman comes looking for Jessup at the house, he tells Ree Jessup Dolly had signed away their home and land. Ree is determined to find her father in the week she has before his court date.<br><br>On her search, Ree descends into the dark secrets of her Ozark people. Blood is not always thicker than water. Thump Milton, the patriarch of the Milton clan will not help her and tells her to abandon her search. Sonny Blond Milton's extent of help is to offer to take in her younger brother Sonny, but not Harold. Seems there was a reason for Sonny being named Sonny, born while Jessup was away in prison. Ree thought Sonny never looked that much like Daddy.<br><br>Winter swirls through the mountains and valleys of the Missouri Ozarks. Ree must take shelter in a cave. An Ozark snow storm will chill you to the bone. She realizes that either she must find her father alive or dead or her family may end up living in one of those caves.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=the_spring.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380393986i/758595.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>You can feel the cold in Woodrell's prose.</i><br><br>Yet, even Uncle Tear Drop will not help her. He takes her to a cabin destroyed by fire, a meth lab destroyed by a cook's mistake. He tells her that Jessup died there. But there is no proof of death.<br><br>Something is terribly wrong. Kin doesn't kill kin except for thievin' and...No her Dad wouldn't ever do that.<br><br>Ree is a heroine, courageous, responsible, and willing to do anything to save her family. Her father must be dead, but she needs proof. As Ree is caught up in swirling violence, frankly, my Dears, I didn't give a damn if he was dead. The guilty always leave the innocent behind.<br><br>Daniel Woodrell knows the Missouri Ozarks. He was born there, grew up there, and lives near the Arkansas line still. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112525.Winter_s_Bone" title="Winter's Bone by Daniel Woodrell" rel="nofollow noopener">Winter's Bone</a>is his eighth novel. Five of his novels have been New York Times Notable Books of the Year. I've got some catching up to do. So, Mr. Woodrell, keep writing. I'm going to be gaining on you.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=Woodrell.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380393986i/758596.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>Daniel Woodrell's lastest book is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10986337.The_Outlaw_Album_Stories" title="The Outlaw Album Stories by Daniel Woodrell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Outlaw Album: Stories</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3773798750184080757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_851945037" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/851945037" />
    <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez"><img alt="Paquita Maria Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594814608p2/2508169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/851945037?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paquita Maria Sanchez" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez">Paquita Maria Sanchez</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340897980" href="/book/show/10765822-winter-s-bone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer851945037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10483476315873932800">Well, I'm an idiot (pretend this is news, please). I dismissed this book out of hand for years because I had a bunch of weird assumptions about it, none of which were based in anything even resembling reality, or the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100812536?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>very encouraging</a> reviews of it I actually did read several years ago. Even despite being told otherwise on multiple occasions, I sorta-thought 1) it was a Young Adult novel, 2) full of cheap emotional ploys, 3) like maybe a teen version of <i>The Lovely Bones</i> only because there's also </span>
  <span id="freeText10483476315873932800" style="display:none">Well, I'm an idiot (pretend this is news, please). I dismissed this book out of hand for years because I had a bunch of weird assumptions about it, none of which were based in anything even resembling reality, or the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100812536?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">very encouraging</a> reviews of it I actually did read several years ago. Even despite being told otherwise on multiple occasions, I sorta-thought 1) it was a Young Adult novel, 2) full of cheap emotional ploys, 3) like maybe a teen version of <i>The Lovely Bones</i> only because there's also a movie of it I've never seen, which has that Hunger Games (a YA novel...right?) girl in it, and 4) because the word "bone" in anything will never pass unnoticed by <i>this</i> teenage boy, but instead will make me lump totally unrelated things together in my head. The lovely boner, winter's boner. I'm not a terribly complex person. As for the YA assumption, I blame <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194036042?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Jason</a> for explicitly stating that was not the case, and in doing so, somehow planting the idea in the back of my stupid brain that it totally was the case. Apparently, I don't listen real good-like, which I also partially blame on the fact that with certain reviewers, I often don't even pay much attention to the title of the book they are reviewing, but just dive right into it because I enjoy their review-writing styles so much. Anyway, as I've mentioned elsewhere, I've been in a noir mood lately (and here is where I stop myself from squeeing some more about that tracking shot in episode four of <i>True Detective</i> and yelling at you for the zillionth time to watch that insanely wonderful show, but seriously: mercy, mercy me), and this book kept popping up as a rec, challenging all my wrongly-wired ideas about it. So I read it. And oh, boyeee.<br /><br />Other people have described the vivid language better than I possibly could, so I won't bother. Much. À la McCarthy, this is a backwoods inferno so leanly yet effectively described that you can smell the blood and shit and rot, and gasp for a couple breaths of clean air in the rare moment where our leading lady is allowed the respite of mental retreat into daydreams, brief, shimmering visions which are themselves shadowed by dread given your knowledge that she is basically dangling just under half her weight over a pit of madness similar to the one fully embraced by her near-catatonic mother. And who wouldn't want to escape this hopeless, woe-filled existence, peopled as it is with impoverished psychopathic rednecks deadened by drugs and drink? A world where scrappiness is both absolutely essential, and will almost certainly get your teeth knocked out at some point. You simply can't win, unless you reality-check the shit out of your notions of victory.<br /><br />I'm not even going to describe the plot, because it is deceptively simple, and this novel deserves more than some boring synopsis. The colorful dialect, the juxtapositions of mostly dark with sporadic, not quite mercifully light imagery, the way this book captures the vicious cycle of violence created and perpetuated by a world where criminality is seen as the only logical solution to poverty. All that. All right here. So good. Almost as good as True De...right, sorry. I shut up now. I grunt, grunt you should read. You read? You read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10483476315873932800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_175991957" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175991957" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175991957?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340897980" href="/book/show/10765822-winter-s-bone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175991957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2690827462334517456">The blood feud world of the sagas in the contemporary Ozarks. Where once people got by through not asking questions and trading in moonshine they now struggle on by not asking questions, and dealing in drugs. The setting is modern rural poverty, but the attitudes and behaviours are redolent of the border reivers. Tough, violent and macho. Whether drugs and alcohol mellow the sharp edges, or simply precipitate the next clash is debatable.<br/><br/><i>One night when Ree was still a bantling Dad had gotten cros</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2690827462334517456" style="display:none">The blood feud world of the sagas in the contemporary Ozarks. Where once people got by through not asking questions and trading in moonshine they now struggle on by not asking questions, and dealing in drugs. The setting is modern rural poverty, but the attitudes and behaviours are redolent of the border reivers. Tough, violent and macho. Whether drugs and alcohol mellow the sharp edges, or simply precipitate the next clash is debatable.<br /><br /><i>One night when Ree was still a bantling Dad had gotten crossways with Buster Leroy Dolly and been shot in the chest clear out by Twin Forks River. He was electric on crank, thrilled to have been shot, and instead of driving to a doctor he drove thirty miles to West Table and the Tiny Spot Tavern to show his assembled buddies the glamorous bullet hole and the blood bubbling. He collapsed grinning and the drunks carried him to the town hospital and nobody thought he'd live to see noon until he did</i> (p29)<br /><br />But this is mere background, what made the book stand out is the language. The distinctive spoken idiom has its own rhythm that sits alongside a variety of authorial voices.<br /><br /><i>Ree followed a path made by prey up hill through scrub, across a bald knob and downhill into a section of pine trees and pine scent and that pious shade and silence pines create. Pine trees with low limbs spread over fresh snow made a stronger vault for the spirit than pews and pulpits ever could. She lingered.</i> (p38)<br /><br />I was carried away by the use of language and am annoyed that I can't find the passage in which Ree, our hero, walks up a snowy hill described in staccato words that become little feet kicking in and pushing the reader up the sharp slope. There was a film made from the book, it was ok as these things go, but to my surprise they made the ending of the film slightly less optimistic than the end of the book and added a scene in which the film-makers made the young woman who is the hero of this tale come across as mildly stupid.<br /><br />This is a book with a simple story. A young woman needs to find her father or else the family home will be lost. However since not asking questions is the one commandment that governs this backwoods chunk of the USA, her quest is bound to lead her into trouble, and it does.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2690827462334517456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_274624616" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/274624616" />
    <a title="B0nnie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7355135-b0nnie"><img alt="B0nnie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325923651p2/7355135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/274624616?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B0nnie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B0nnie" href="/user/show/7355135-b0nnie">B0nnie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780316007382" href="/book/show/9916258-winter-s-bone">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer274624616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3955674338133954679"><br/> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380369376i/722163._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='230' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Beautifully written, this is a simple story about survival, winter and bones. The main thing to survive is poverty - the kind where buttonless overcoats are de rigueur, and hunting and skinning squirrels is not done strictly for entertainment. It's in the Ozarks and winter is bone-cracking cold. We open the book to a hint of flurries, and venison hanging in trees to "sweeten that meat to the bone", and we meet Ree Dolly, our tough teen heroine. <br/><br/>The title "Winter's Bone" for me summoned a cold, </span>
  <span id="freeText3955674338133954679" style="display:none"><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380369376i/722163._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="230" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Beautifully written, this is a simple story about survival, winter and bones. The main thing to survive is poverty - the kind where buttonless overcoats are de rigueur, and hunting and skinning squirrels is not done strictly for entertainment. It's in the Ozarks and winter is bone-cracking cold. We open the book to a hint of flurries, and venison hanging in trees to "sweeten that meat to the bone", and we meet Ree Dolly, our tough teen heroine. <br><br>The title "Winter's Bone" for me summoned a cold, terrible image: blue, white, visceral, and hard. It is actually the perfect mental picture for this story. The real meaning is something quite different, but hey misunderstanding titles (and lyrics) is what I do. <br><br> Bones. Bones. Dem bones is everywhere. There's the olden Dolly kin "who had so many bones that broke, broke and mended, broke and mended wrong, so they limped through life on the bad-mend bones for year upon year until falling dead in a single evening from something that sounded wet in the lungs". There's the fun bones, "Lust slaking to dance tunes, standing hip bone to hip bone, the new hands moving over her rumples and furls and tender knobs, hands good as tongues in the dark corners of those whiskey moments", and the tasty bones, "Soup stock from deer bones simmered on the stove and steamed a comforting scent."<br><br>Meth cooking is the main industry in these parts. It isn't all "let's party", and it isn't blue. But it is of the "I am the one who knocks" variety.<br><br> Sometimes safety at the workplace fails and everything blows, and the makeshift lab is no longer viable. "That shit's all poison, girl. Toxic. It'll eat the skin clean off your bones and wilt the bones, too. It'll turn your lungs to paper sacks and tear holes in 'em". <br><br>Towards the end, when Ree finds what she is looking for, I think there was a sort of failure of nerves on the part of the author. "Is my story too quietly grim", he seems to have asked himself, "or could it use some CSI graphic osteology, with chain saw and axe?" <br><br>I would much prefer the quietly grim over the sensational. <br><br>Really, all that's needed is a <a href="http://youtu.be/dXiRaOe5z_s" rel="nofollow noopener">Hardscrabble Elegy.</a><br><br><br><br> <br><br> <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="3955674338133954679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_53404062" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53404062" />
    <a title="D. Pow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow"><img alt="D. Pow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339098707p2/2182799.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53404062?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D. Pow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="D. Pow" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow">D. Pow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316066419" href="/book/show/1696301.Winter_s_Bone">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53404062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2391128109608252652">Man, O Man, can this guy write. This is a very impressive novel. Here is language that soars, home-spun lyricisms, trailer-trash poetry, a book chock-full of crackhead sonnet riffs; Woodrell is a virtuoso of the first degree. <br/><br/>In Ree Dolly, the teenage protagonist he has conjured up, he has invented somebody you'll remember gladly until your dying days. Fiercely courageous with a keen eye for the moral effrontery foisted on her small shoulders by kin close and distant, she is feisty as a stirred-</span>
  <span id="freeText2391128109608252652" style="display:none">Man, O Man, can this guy write. This is a very impressive novel. Here is language that soars, home-spun lyricisms, trailer-trash poetry, a book chock-full of crackhead sonnet riffs; Woodrell is a virtuoso of the first degree. <br /><br />In Ree Dolly, the teenage protagonist he has conjured up, he has invented somebody you'll remember gladly until your dying days. Fiercely courageous with a keen eye for the moral effrontery foisted on her small shoulders by kin close and distant, she is feisty as a stirred-up Wolverine and wiser than her elders by far. Instances of unsurpassed brutality and the banality of poverty vie with quiet moments of grace and communion with nature wherein Ree’s beat down body and unbeatable spirit serve as host to all that could be perceived as transcendent in this often unfair and fucked up world. <br /><br />Loved the book. Love Woodrell. Loved Ree. Sheee-it, folks, read it for yourself and see. You’ll love it too.<br /><br /><br />Note: Thanks to Ben Harrison for recovering this review. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2391128109608252652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145802990?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316131612" href="/book/show/8465248-winter-s-bone">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">trilobites</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1910836-lobstergirl?shelf=to-be-burned">to-be-burned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1910836-lobstergirl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145802990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8854301491704855982">Horrendous, goopy, writers' workshop writing.<br/><br/>"Moons of ache glowed in spaces of her meat and when she moved the moons banged together and stunned." (Are sentences required to make sense in "contemporary fiction?")<br/><br/>"Moans droned from her chest of bones. Shit leaked from her panties and she felt runnels of yuck on her thighs." Channeling Dr. Seuss and Cormac McCarthy simultaneously: ambitious!<br/><br/>"She thrust her head into the cold and broadcast the hot mush of old swallowed food toward the snowbanks." </span>
  <span id="freeText8854301491704855982" style="display:none">Horrendous, goopy, writers' workshop writing.<br /><br />"Moons of ache glowed in spaces of her meat and when she moved the moons banged together and stunned." (Are sentences required to make sense in "contemporary fiction?")<br /><br />"Moans droned from her chest of bones. Shit leaked from her panties and she felt runnels of yuck on her thighs." Channeling Dr. Seuss and Cormac McCarthy simultaneously: ambitious!<br /><br />"She thrust her head into the cold and broadcast the hot mush of old swallowed food toward the snowbanks." Conjures up a Rush Limbaugh radio program, somehow. Was "catapulted" too literal?<br /><br />The thought that instructors are actually encouraging soupy messes like this is dispiriting, and one wants now to read things that are cleansing, purifying, bracing tonics, literary enemas - Willa Cather, Sir Walter Scott, Jackie Collins, the minutes of the winter meeting of the Financial Accounting Standards Board.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8854301491704855982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_668411130" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/668411130" />

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/668411130?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=read-for-work">read-for-work</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=modern-fiction">modern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=midwest">midwest</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer668411130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4637718538445441842">This novella is a gem. It's tough yet emotional, scary yet empowering, stark and yet beautiful.<br/><br/>I have been meaning to read <i>Winter's Bone</i> for several years, but I kept putting it off because I thought it would be depressing. And while the story is bleak, it is so gorgeously written that I got lost in the prose. <br/><br/>The book is set in an impoverished region in southern Missouri called the Ozarks, where making meth is a popular way of earning a living. But 17-year-old Ree Dolly hopes to escape to the A</span>
  <span id="freeText4637718538445441842" style="display:none">This novella is a gem. It's tough yet emotional, scary yet empowering, stark and yet beautiful.<br /><br />I have been meaning to read <i>Winter's Bone</i> for several years, but I kept putting it off because I thought it would be depressing. And while the story is bleak, it is so gorgeously written that I got lost in the prose. <br /><br />The book is set in an impoverished region in southern Missouri called the Ozarks, where making meth is a popular way of earning a living. But 17-year-old Ree Dolly hopes to escape to the Army next year, if she can track down her Daddy. He's out on bond and has been gone for a while, and if she doesn't find him, she'll lose the family home.<br /><br />Poor Ree. She's just a teenager, yet she has to make all of the adult decisions because her mother has gone crazy and her daddy skipped out. She's even taking care of her two younger brothers, teaching them how to cook, how to fight, and how to shoot.<br /><br />Although friends advise her against it, Ree starts asking around to see if anyone can find her father. The more she asks, the more danger she finds. This is one of those stories where a happy ending seems impossible. <br /><br />The movie version of this book with Jennifer Lawrence is well done, but I thought the book was much richer in detail and description. I highly recommend it.<br /><br /><b>Favorite Descriptions</b><br />"Mom sat in her chair beside the potbelly and the boys sat at the table eating what Ree fed them. Mom's morning pills turned her into a cat, a breathing thing that sat near heat and occasionally made a sound. Mom's chair was an old padded rocker that seldom rocked, and at odd instants she'd hum ill-matched snips of music, notes unrelated by melody or pitch. But for most of any day she was quiet and still, wearing a small lingering smile prompted by something vaguely nice going on inside her head. She was a Bromont, born to this house, and she'd once been pretty. Even as she was now, medicated and lost to the present, with hair she forgot to wash or brush and deep wrinkles growing on her face, you could see she'd once been as comely as any girl that ever danced barefoot across this tangled country of Ozark hills and hollers. Long, dark, and lovely she had been, in those days before her mind broke and the parts scattered and she let them go."<br /><br />"Ree's grand hope was that these boys would not be dead to wonder by age twelve, dulled to life, empty of kindness, boiling with mean. So many Dolly kids were that way, ruined before they had chin hair, groomed to live outside square law and abide by the remorseless blood-soaked commandments that governed lives led outside square law."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4637718538445441842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101398004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101398004" />
    <a title="RandomAnthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony"><img alt="RandomAnthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470919631p2/721021.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101398004?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RandomAnthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RandomAnthony" href="/user/show/721021-randomanthony">RandomAnthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316066419" href="/book/show/1696301.Winter_s_Bone">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101398004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1631972935961140714">Ok, I read <i>Winter's Bone</i> on D and Karen's recommendations, so I’m posting links to their reviews before I start:<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53404062'>http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br/><br/>and<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100812536'>http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br/><br/><i>Winter’s Bone</i> is a hell of a book in that A) the novel is fantastic, and B) it's set in an American version of hell. The story of Ree, a teenage girl charged with finding her bail-jumping father in order to save her family’s house, catches fire early and never cools down. Ree lives in a terrifying sectio</span>
  <span id="freeText1631972935961140714" style="display:none">Ok, I read <i>Winter's Bone</i> on D and Karen's recommendations, so I’m posting links to their reviews before I start:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53404062">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /><br />and<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100812536">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /><br /><i>Winter’s Bone</i> is a hell of a book in that A) the novel is fantastic, and B) it's set in an American version of hell. The story of Ree, a teenage girl charged with finding her bail-jumping father in order to save her family’s house, catches fire early and never cools down. Ree lives in a terrifying section of the Ozarks that takes on the character of a mythical underworld with its own set of unwritten codes and rules, of which the two most important are “don’t snitch” and “don’t steal”. This book made me want to never drive through the Ozarks again. Seriously. What if your car broke down? The characters in <i>Winter’s Bone</i> made the guys from <i>Deliverance</i> look like pussies. Ree must traverse this hellish landscape of tenacious loyalties and inbred family ties with the help of her baby-bound former best friend and a mutilated uncle flying on crank. Good luck with that, eh?<br /><br />By the way, I’m not exaggerating when I describe this book as “mythical”. Three grim, violent sisters reminded me of the fates and furies. Other characters were monstrous sociopaths. Ree’s gripping journey has an epic quality; she’ll cross hell to preserve her brothers’ redemption and reclaim hope and dignity. Woodrell’s brilliant phrasing captures the dread winter in the kind of place where everyone knows each other and there’s no comfortable way to reconcile the idea that you share a country with the natives. He catches details that color in the lines of despair and unspoken unease. Ree’s resolve is all the more paradoxically human and superhuman for the surroundings. <br /><br />	This book checks in at less than 200 pages but feels complete and more than satisfying. Goddamn, I loved <i>Winter’s Bone</i>. Now I want to see the movie. I hope they don’t fuck it up. Mr. Woodrell’s sparse, muscular prose deserves all the praise and awards around its neck and a solid movie to match. <br /><br />(Thanks, D. and Karen!) <br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1631972935961140714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2624672-tk421?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182231432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17798906595874338220">A few authors over the course of the past few years have recently stood out above the normal, literary crowd—for me, anyways. And the thing I noticed about these authors is that they all seem to write darker fiction. If I had to say, a good representation of these authors is: Roberto Bolano, Cormac McCarthy, Castle Freeman, Jr., and now, Daniel Woodrell. <br/><br/>These authors make up a class of writers that I have termed Brutal Poets. Their use of language invokes a visceral response from the reader, so</span>
  <span id="freeText17798906595874338220" style="display:none">A few authors over the course of the past few years have recently stood out above the normal, literary crowd—for me, anyways. And the thing I noticed about these authors is that they all seem to write darker fiction. If I had to say, a good representation of these authors is: Roberto Bolano, Cormac McCarthy, Castle Freeman, Jr., and now, Daniel Woodrell. <br /><br />These authors make up a class of writers that I have termed Brutal Poets. Their use of language invokes a visceral response from the reader, sounds sometimes biblical or archaic, but never forced. The way they describe a landscape that mimics the inner feelings of characters is alarming and disorientating because of its precision and perfection. Basically, these authors seem to really know what it is that they are writing about. <br /><br />Woodrell, a previous unknown to me before I saw the adaptation of his novel on DVD, is a master storyteller. Terse and sparse, his novel WINTER’S BONE is a punishing reward of a read. The story takes place over a few days in the Ozarks, Appalachia country. Ree Dolly needs to find Jessup, her daddy. He’s skipped out on court, and has put his house and acreage up for collateral for his bond. The problem is something isn’t right. He’s gone missing. And Ree begins to understand more about where she lives exactly, and who the people really are that populate her family and community. Characters like Thump and Uncle Teardrop and even Ree’s Mom add a depth to this story that brings it past a “missing-person” story or a “murder mystery.” <br /><br />From the moment Ree takes it upon herself to find out the truth about her father, to the moment she has the emotional revelation at the end of the story with Uncle Teardrop, this bleak novel will keep you mesmerized, wanting to turn pages as fast as you can until you have reached the staggering and horrifying conclusion. <br /><br />Don’t do this. Don’t ruin such a great experience by consuming it whole. Nibble at the book. Let your mind digest it. Savor it. Like a death row inmate being given their last meal, taste every juicy word on every page. Drink up every image and scene. There are plenty of other books out there to scarf down as fast as you can. Not this one.<br /><br />Warning: be prepared. Oftentimes there are bleak moments like when Ree is beaten by the Milton sisters, and presented in a barn to Thump. When Ree comes to, she and a girl named Megan exchange these words:<br /><br />Megan squatted, patted Ree’s face, and said, “Whatever are we to do about you, baby girl? Huh?”<br /><br />“Kill me, I guess.”<br /><br />“That idea has been said already. Got’ny other ones?”<br /><br />“Help me. Ain’t nobody said that idea yet, have they?”<br /><br />This scene still makes my flesh get goose bumps. <br /><br /><br />(VERY HIGHEST RECOMMENDATION) <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17798906595874338220" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170211577?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Thane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Thane" href="/user/show/3483907-james-thane">James Thane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170211577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12899264857193536974">Sixteen-year-old Ree Dolly lives for the day when she will be old enough to join the Army and escape the grinding economic and intellectual poverty of her life in the Missouri Ozarks where her extended family lives by a variety of illegal pursuits, mostly involving the manufacture and distribution of methamphetamines and crank cocaine. But Ree's dreams are shattered when her father, a celebrated meth chef, disappears, leaving Ree nearly penniless and responsible for her two younger brothers and </span>
  <span id="freeText12899264857193536974" style="display:none">Sixteen-year-old Ree Dolly lives for the day when she will be old enough to join the Army and escape the grinding economic and intellectual poverty of her life in the Missouri Ozarks where her extended family lives by a variety of illegal pursuits, mostly involving the manufacture and distribution of methamphetamines and crank cocaine. But Ree's dreams are shattered when her father, a celebrated meth chef, disappears, leaving Ree nearly penniless and responsible for her two younger brothers and her mentally-ill mother.<br /><br />The situation becomes even worse when Ree discovers that, before disappearing, her father had posted bail using the family's house as collateral. If he fails to appear in court a week later, as will almost certainly be the case, Ree, her mother and brothers will lose everything. Ree thus embarks on a desperate search to find her father, dead or alive, before the court date. She is thwarted at almost every turn by her own relatives and by the code of silence that pervades the culture around her. And when Ree ignores repeated warnings to abandon her search, she pays a fearsome price.<br /><br />Woodrell paints a vivid and heart-wrenching portrait of life in the Ozarks that has the ring of a desperate truth. In Ree Dolly, he has created a character that reminds one of Mattie Ross in Charles Portis's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/257845.True_Grit" title="True Grit by Charles Portis" rel="nofollow noopener">True Grit</a>. But Ree's mission takes her into a much darker world that seems to have few redeeming features and very little hope. And unfortunately for Ree, Rooster Cogburn will not be riding to the rescue.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12899264857193536974" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_829268933" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/829268933" />
    <a title="Anthony Vacca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27183668-anthony-vacca"><img alt="Anthony Vacca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604067844p2/27183668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/829268933?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anthony Vacca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anthony Vacca" href="/user/show/27183668-anthony-vacca">Anthony Vacca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27183668-anthony-vacca?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27183668-anthony-vacca?shelf=southern-fiction">southern-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer829268933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6844028316002550395">For the most part, I am perfectly content to read books littered with despicable exemplars of our wonderful species. Give me your moral degenerates, your psychopathic wives, your misogynistic husbands, your bloodthirsty children, your lechers, repeat killers, serial adulterers, conmen, thieves, necrophiliacs, Christians—give me every last bit of your human refuge because I will gladly read about them over honest, good, hardworking men and women any day of the week. Why? Because good people are b</span>
  <span id="freeText6844028316002550395" style="display:none">For the most part, I am perfectly content to read books littered with despicable exemplars of our wonderful species. Give me your moral degenerates, your psychopathic wives, your misogynistic husbands, your bloodthirsty children, your lechers, repeat killers, serial adulterers, conmen, thieves, necrophiliacs, Christians—give me every last bit of your human refuge because I will gladly read about them over honest, good, hardworking men and women any day of the week. Why? Because good people are boring outside of the real world.<br /><br />So it was a bit of a surprise to get knocked arse over teakettle off my high horse of arch cynicism by this mean, gaunt and big-hearted novel by Daniel Woodrell, the esteemed bard of anti-bucolicism in the Ozarks. <br /><br /><i>Winter’s Bone</i> relates the story of 16-year old Ree Dolly and her quest to find her missing father—an all-around fuck-up who is good for one thing, and that is making meth—before he misses his court date and the gov’ment takes possession of the family’s house and land, which good ole dad was kind enough to put up so he could make bail and then split. Add a nearly catatonic mom and two younger brothers who completely rely on their older sis for survival, and you’d be kind of a cold bitch or bastard to not feel something for Ree. But what really got under my skin was seeing how obviously foiled Ree’s life and dreams already are at the age most of us think we got this whole life situation on lockdown. Woodrell brings to life the hollers and frozen wastelands of the novel’s setting, packed with their hundreds upon hundreds of kissing cousins that have been loafing about since for God knows how many generations now. Ree lives in a patriarchal shithole of a society that champions lifelessness, despair, violence, teen pregnancy, and drug abuse over education, happiness, and self-worth. And a poor economy is only somewhat to blame for these factors.<br /><br />But Woodrell’s novel functions as more than just a condemnation of American backwoods life and the banal cruelty humans enact upon one another; it also offers something resembling hope to the reader, even if it is the meager chance that humans may actually be capable of caring about other people. And let the record show that Ree is a heroine to marvel over as you root for her to somehow come out on top by the end of this sparse, hauntingly-written Gothic yarn. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6844028316002550395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_203379463" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203379463" />
    <a title="Trudi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi"><img alt="Trudi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203379463?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudi" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi">Trudi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/9836858-winter-s-bone">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=a-sense-of-place">a-sense-of-place</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=all-in-the-family">all-in-the-family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=my-desert-island">my-desert-island</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203379463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7416226918799188394">I've put off writing a review for this book because I always struggle with the great ones and Woodrell's <i>Winter's Bone</i> is one of those (with a capital G). It's craft and heart and drama and beauty. It's poetry and grit, entangled in an embrace of love and hatred. <br/><br/>Woodrell offers up a stinging portrait of impoverished life in the Ozarks, where kin saves as often as it condemns. The hill people of Ree's world live by their own laws separate from that of the state -- of paramount importance, don't </span>
  <span id="freeText7416226918799188394" style="display:none">I've put off writing a review for this book because I always struggle with the great ones and Woodrell's <i>Winter's Bone</i> is one of those (with a capital G). It's craft and heart and drama and beauty. It's poetry and grit, entangled in an embrace of love and hatred. <br /><br />Woodrell offers up a stinging portrait of impoverished life in the Ozarks, where kin saves as often as it condemns. The hill people of Ree's world live by their own laws separate from that of the state -- of paramount importance, don't be a snitch and mind your own business. Bad things happen to anyone who talks too much or asks too many questions. Unfortunately, sixteen year old Ree has <i>a lot</i> of questions that need answering with only her to ask them. Left on her own to protect a shattered mother and two helpless kid brothers, Ree is desperate to uncover the whereabouts of her meth-making father. She must venture into the cold and ice and pass over hostile thresholds where she is neither invited nor wanted. <br /><br />Ree’s fierceness and courage stole my heart. She ranks as one of my favorite literary characters OF ALL TIME. Her stubbornness and smart mouth made me smile as much as it made me fear for her safety. Ree has her own set of rules to live by that include, stepping in to do for her brothers where her parents have failed and “Never. Never ask for what ought to be offered.” Ree is an old soul, mature beyond her years, forced to grow up fast and smart in a world that has teeth and a taste for blood. <br /><br />This is a harsh story, one where the author pulls no punches. Woodrell is not out to romanticize this hill life or the hardscrabble characters living it. He wants us to see the ugly, to <i>feel</i> it in our bones, but for all of that there is tremendous beauty here as well, not just in the prose that SINGS but in the simplicity of a proud people who do what they must to survive in an environment that does not forgive weakness or stupidity lightly.<br /><br />I cannot recommend this book enough. I am also going to recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143971062" rel="nofollow noopener">Kemper’s review here</a>, because he does such a wonderful job capturing the book’s honesty and intensity. If I haven’t convinced you to read <i>Winter’s Bone</i>, he will. <br /><br /><b>***A note on the audio version:</b> Outstanding! Emma Galvin captures Ree’s strength and vulnerability perfectly. Woodrell’s prose is so gorgeous it soars when read aloud. <blockquote>Love and hate hold hands always so it made natural sense that they'd get confused by upset married folk in the wee hours once in a while and a nosebleed or bruised breast might result. But it just seemed proof that a great foulness was afoot in the world when a no-strings roll in the hay with a stranger led to chipped teeth or cigarette burns on the wrist. `<i>Winter's Bone</i></blockquote> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7416226918799188394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28488153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4833272262993968422">Great story, characterful, fits in with my favorite authors Cormac McCarthy and Annie Proulx. I loved Ree! Hear a movie is made of this book, glad I read the book first!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1096650875" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1096650875" />
    <a title="LeAnne: GeezerMom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7862560-leanne-geezermom"><img alt="LeAnne: GeezerMom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514579326p2/7862560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1096650875?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="LeAnne: GeezerMom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="LeAnne: GeezerMom" href="/user/show/7862560-leanne-geezermom">LeAnne: GeezerMom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7862560-leanne-geezermom?shelf=favorite-books">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7862560-leanne-geezermom?shelf=litmus-test">litmus-test</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1096650875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11544731107753508487">Icy, dark, beautiful, and brutal. Ive rarely hurt for and hoped for a character as much as I did Ree. Sweet Pea, for all time. <br/><br/>The main character here is written as tough and tender and backed into a hard place, and it is terrific to see a strong female protagonist in a work of Southern grit lit. Ree is a bright teen living in poverty but who sees a sensible way out of the awful lifestyle that her parents have accepted. <br/><br/>As her plans start to gel, a family emergency pops up, and there are no adul</span>
  <span id="freeText11544731107753508487" style="display:none">Icy, dark, beautiful, and brutal. Ive rarely hurt for and hoped for a character as much as I did Ree. Sweet Pea, for all time. <br /><br />The main character here is written as tough and tender and backed into a hard place, and it is terrific to see a strong female protagonist in a work of Southern grit lit. Ree is a bright teen living in poverty but who sees a sensible way out of the awful lifestyle that her parents have accepted. <br /><br />As her plans start to gel, a family emergency pops up, and there are no adults who can manage the crisis. Although she can carry on with her own plans, instead she chooses to stare danger in the face and take heroic action. If she does not take the risks she does, her siblings and mentally incapable mother will suffer.<br /><br />What is so amazing about Daniel Woodrell's writing - his stuff is very gritty, super southern noir -is that he steps seamlessly inside his female protagonists to look out from their eyes. In Tomato Red, there are two tough cookies - a mom &amp; her maybe 19 year old daughter. As he does here with the girl named Ree, Woodrell speaks for them with incredible skill. So very rare for somebody to write both genders so well.<br /><br />There is nothing smiley-faced or sunshiney about this novel. The descriptions of blighted characters, stark country side, and danger are phenomenal - but it is the sweetness this girl feels for her loved ones and her courage that will make you root for her. If you loved the fictional girls called Scout or Swede or Swan, then this is the powerful teenager she could have grown into if her upbringing were as harsh. Loved this book. 5 stars and on my favorites shelf. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11544731107753508487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2623175684" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer4031005328846810487">I'm not feeling like a full review today so I'll limit this to only a few comments. <br/><br/>*The Ozarks in which this book takes place seem to have nothing in common with the OZARK Netflix show. <br/><br/>*I have no doubt in my mind that life in some areas of the Ozarks is as brutal as it's depicted in this book. Poverty, drug use, tight family units, and long-held multi-generational grudges are just part of the miserable lives examined here. <br/><br/>*I couldn't help but feel for 16 year old Ree who just wanted to join t</span>
  <span id="freeText4031005328846810487" style="display:none">I'm not feeling like a full review today so I'll limit this to only a few comments. <br /><br />*The Ozarks in which this book takes place seem to have nothing in common with the OZARK Netflix show. <br /><br />*I have no doubt in my mind that life in some areas of the Ozarks is as brutal as it's depicted in this book. Poverty, drug use, tight family units, and long-held multi-generational grudges are just part of the miserable lives examined here. <br /><br />*I couldn't help but feel for 16 year old Ree who just wanted to join the army and get the hell out of there. Due to her mother's mental illness and her two young siblings, her hands were tied. It's hard to escape family. <br /><br />*I thought this book was savage with sharp, vivid prose-sometimes so sharp it stabbed me right in the heart. <br /><br />*I enjoyed WINTER'S BONE, as much as one can enjoy a story this violent and merciless. I look forward to sampling more of Daniel Woodrell's work in the future. <br /><br />*Recommended for those with the wherewithal to stomach the brutalities of this rural, mountain life. You have been warned!</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1625173834" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1625173834" />
    <a title="Puck" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18562856-puck"><img alt="Puck" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498403772p2/18562856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1625173834?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Puck" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Puck" href="/user/show/18562856-puck">Puck</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340897980" href="/book/show/10765822-winter-s-bone">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Annie Proulx (&quot;Brokeback Mountain&quot;) </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=favorites-2017">favorites-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=underrated">underrated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18562856-puck?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1625173834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer788309208895913080"><b>A perfect, ice-cold winter read that burns like frostbite, but in a pleasant way. </b><br/><br/>In this thriller set in the harsh Ozark Mountains, 17-year-old Ree Dolly is determined to find her father. Deadbeat dad and meth-cooker Jessup Dolly didn’t show up for court, and since he put up their house for collateral with the bailbondsmen, chances are high that the family gets kicked out by the end of the month. <br/>Bearing responsibility for her two younger brothers and her mentally unstable mother, Ree starts as</span>
  <span id="freeText788309208895913080" style="display:none"><b>A perfect, ice-cold winter read that burns like frostbite, but in a pleasant way. </b><br /><br />In this thriller set in the harsh Ozark Mountains, 17-year-old Ree Dolly is determined to find her father. Deadbeat dad and meth-cooker Jessup Dolly didn’t show up for court, and since he put up their house for collateral with the bailbondsmen, chances are high that the family gets kicked out by the end of the month. <br />Bearing responsibility for her two younger brothers and her mentally unstable mother, Ree starts asking questions – expect people in Ozark <b>hate</b> questions. Everyone living in the poor mountain community, from friends to (distant) family members, tell her to shut up and don’t cause trouble, but Ree can’t listen: she just has too much to lose. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i> “Jessup never would smack you. I don’t know why, why he never would, but I always have said someday somebody’s goin’ to pay a price for him not whompin’ you good when you needed it.”<br />“I wasn’t tryin’ to be a smart mouth, there, Teardrop. Uncle Teardrop.”<br />“It don’t seem like you’ve got to try none, girl, smarty-mouth shit just flies out your yap anytime your yap falls open.” </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Ree is an incredible heroine. She’s a prime example of a tough female character: brave, independent, resourceful, smart, but not afraid to be kind and tender to her loved-ones. <i> “She would never cry where her tears might be seen and counted against her”, </i> but in the moments where Ree is together with her brothers or her best friend Gail, you see her hidden soft side and warm heart. <br /><br />However, due to what life has given her, she had to grow grim and cold; it’s all a matter of survival. Ree is a young woman – a teenager, still - carrying a heavy load while living in a harsh, cruel, and unforgiving world without any laws or people to help her. <i> “You should never ask for what ought to be offered”,</i> but not even family bonds guarantee support when you’re living in Ozark and decide to get into other people’s business. <br />Ree’s uncle Teardrop is a great illustration of these peculiar (family) dynamics between people: the man himself has half of his face melted off, he’s a crystal meth addict and he is basically a very dangerous person. Ree is terrified of him (and she should be), but later on you learn how he cares for her, in his own strange way. <br /><br />So this book isn’t a cheerful read. It’s bleak and grim and although you’ll love Ree’s unbending spirit, your heart will break for the price she has to pay to discover the truth about her missing father. <br />The story is therefore pretty scary and emotional and can sometimes feel quite hopeless, but reading Ree’s conversations with her brothers, Gail, or her uncle will give your strength. Ree is on her own but not alone, and even though the ending of the book isn’t happy, you get the feeling Ree will be okay.<br /><br />I take one star off because sometimes the descriptions of the winter landscape were a little too wordy and the pace getting too slow around 60% of the book, but overall this is an incredible read to start 2017 with, so <b>4 stars</b>. <br /><br />PS. In 2010 “Winter’s Bone” was made into a movie with the same name, and the book-to-movie-adaptation is, in my opinion, very well done. The movie stays loyal to the plot, the atmosphere and the setting are perfect, and the portrayal of Ree Dolly by a very young Jennifer Lawrence is fantastic. If you like this book, you’ll definitely like the movie too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="788309208895913080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ti6Pck5spUgJB5bBJBWr2598Tq5Ktpt8QyfwxvWtvD3/gZSCMHqjaRulIeF+anc8ahQlmgO7CG8Gtbh3l0k4Cg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QBPtubQdGi3EPnCEnVw70vX048Ti00Z4RQcwTYLG2HbxvPZJygscDNacx6THI+c1AJyI8Kve1WsAlXj84CJcQQ==')})
  }

A father pumps his son full of steroids so he can vicariously relive his days as a perpetual runner-up body builder. A psychotic rural recluse comes upon two siblings committing incest and feels compelled to take action.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12436945940263551516"><i>Growing up in Missouri, seventy miles downriver from Hannibal, Mark Twain was handed to me early on, first or second grade, and captivated me for years, and forever, I reckon. Robert Louis Stevenson had his seasons with me just before my teens and I love him yet. There are too many others to mention, I suppose, but feel compelled to bring up Hemingway, James Agee, Flannery O'Connor, John McGahern,</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12436945940263551516" style="display:none"><i>Growing up in Missouri, seventy miles downriver from Hannibal, Mark Twain was handed to me early on, first or second grade, and captivated me for years, and forever, I reckon. Robert Louis Stevenson had his seasons with me just before my teens and I love him yet. There are too many others to mention, I suppose, but feel compelled to bring up Hemingway, James Agee, Flannery O'Connor, John McGahern, Knut Hamsun, Faulkner, George Mackay Brown, Tillie Olsen, W.S. Merwin, Brigit Pegeen Kelly, Andrew Hudgins, Seamus Heaney, Derek Wolco.</i><br /><br />Daniel Woodrell was born and now lives in the Missouri Ozarks. He left school and enlisted in the Marines the week he turned seventeen, received his bachelor's degree at age twenty-seven, graduated from the Iowa Writer's Workshop, and spent a year on a Michener Fellowship. His five most recent novels were selected as New York Times Notable Books of the Year, and <i>Tomato Red</i> won the PEN West award for the novel in 1999. <i>Winter's Bone</i> is his eighth novel.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12436945940263551516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
